  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c43423c4-b758-4622-a4b9-ef7ff629cff3'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer7790405322010100698">There is September 11 and then there are the days after, and finally the years.<br/><br/>Falling Man is a magnificent, essential novel about the event that defines turn-of-the-century America. It begins in the smoke and ash of the burning towers and tracks the aftermath of this global tremor in the intimate lives of a few people.<br/><br/>First there is Keith, walking out of the rubble into a</span>
  <span id="freeText7790405322010100698" style="display:none">There is September 11 and then there are the days after, and finally the years.<br /><br />Falling Man is a magnificent, essential novel about the event that defines turn-of-the-century America. It begins in the smoke and ash of the burning towers and tracks the aftermath of this global tremor in the intimate lives of a few people.<br /><br />First there is Keith, walking out of the rubble into a life that he'd always imagined belonged to everyone but him. Then Lianne, his estranged wife, memory-haunted, trying to reconcile two versions of the same shadowy man. And their small son Justin, standing at the window, scanning the sky for more planes.<br /><br />These are lives choreographed by loss, grief and the enormous force of history.</span>

<a href="/questions/382385-i-didn-t-read-the-book-yet-but-i-d-like">I didn&#39;t read the book yet, but I&#39;d like to know is there any Islamophobia in it and if so where is it mentioned?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_382385'><a id="like_count_community_question_382385" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/382385?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F28700.Falling_Man">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/54560582-edi"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/54560582-edi">Edi</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'd question what you mean by asking this, what your intentions are towards this novel. DeLillo is one of the world's finest current writers. If there<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'd question what you mean by asking this, what your intentions are towards this novel. DeLillo is one of the world's finest current writers. If there is 'islamophobia' in it, or homophobia, or transphobia, those would be views expressed by the characters in the novel. Isn't that the point of novels? To present differing perspectives, experiences, prejudices, narratives? Given this is a 9/11 novel, you might expect some of the protagonists to present 'islamophobic' views (by which I take it you mean criticism of Islam). Given so many people did. In answer to your question, there is none. But find out for yourself: You take yourself into a novel, your expectations, your experiences, your '-phobias', your prejudices. That's the point. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<a href="/questions/431248-why-do-you-think-it-got-only-3-19-stars-on">Why do you think it got only 3,19 stars on average?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_431248'><a id="like_count_community_question_431248" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/431248?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F28700.Falling_Man">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/2400951-kallie"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1249764384i/2400951._UY100_CR25,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/2400951-kallie">Kallie</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I am 2/3 of the way through Falling Man and plan to re-read it. De Lillo as always writes a novel in poetic language, and looks beyond reassuring clic<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I am 2/3 of the way through Falling Man and plan to re-read it. De Lillo as always writes a novel in poetic language, and looks beyond reassuring cliches about personal connection, into the voids we prefer not to see and the physical presence we prefer to avoid because being in that reminds us: this is finite and we don't know what comes after. His writing, as always if I engage with it, alters my state of awareness and perception of what I take for granted. But the process of connecting with his work can take some time and surrender. Every word matters. If the reader refuses to enter the story on De Lillo's uncompromising terms, they will miss the experience of this novel -- and it's not just about a story, but the experience of being in these characters' minds. It's sort of a paradox, in that he shows the unbridgeable space between people, yet takes us more intimately into people's uncensored thoughts and perceptions. But we have to want to go there and not everyone does; most (and I include myself in this, a lot of the time) want 'a story about . . .,' which is quite a different, lulling and distracting experience.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_4632682" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4632682" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6896-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1176778349p2/6896.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4632682?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/6896-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4632682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17188318316528012202">Don DeLillo's novel <i>Falling Man</i> has more unspecified pronouns than I care to read. <br/><br/>It's written in that postmodern style that calls for rapidly changing vignettes; the reader bounces from one scene to another to another in just four pages, and as if to drive us mad, DeLillo hardly ever tells us who is speaking or acting. The sections begin with sentences like: "He missed the kid" or "She missed those nights with friends when you talk about everything." We're left in the dark, and the characters,</span>
  <span id="freeText17188318316528012202" style="display:none">Don DeLillo's novel <i>Falling Man</i> has more unspecified pronouns than I care to read. <br /><br />It's written in that postmodern style that calls for rapidly changing vignettes; the reader bounces from one scene to another to another in just four pages, and as if to drive us mad, DeLillo hardly ever tells us who is speaking or acting. The sections begin with sentences like: "He missed the kid" or "She missed those nights with friends when you talk about everything." We're left in the dark, and the characters, as a result, feel far, far away.<br /><br />Maybe this is the point. Maybe. This is, after all, a novel about a dysfunctional family (father, mother, son) and the impact of 9/11 on their lives. The novel begins in the cloudy streets after the attack, and it jumps around through the preceding events and the aftermath until it finally returns to the husband's experience inside the towers when the planes hit. The characters are already estranged from each other before they suffer this trauma, and the events that follow only further estrange them.<br /><br />And so, it seems, nobody really knows anybody, and hardly anyone really wants to talk to anyone else about anything, and DeLillo, it seems, wants us to feel this emptiness and helplessness. Conversations between characters are abstract and oblique. They repeat lines often, as if in a trance. Their sentences fall short. They all fade away from each other. Sometimes they even talk about fading away from each other. <br /><br />Maybe this is an accurate portrayal of the shock and confusion of what happened, but it doesn't make good reading. <br /><br />The question it raises, though, is what DeLillo's intention is. Reading this was maddening; there's very little to grab onto. If his goal is for us to experience the same aimless wandering as the people who walked away from the rubble, then he has succeeded. We're confused, we don't know where we're going, but we keep turning the pages.<br /><br />Unfortunately, doing so is a burden. I wanted again and again to abandon this book, but forced myself through it. The ending, however, did offer some reward. In the final pages DeLillo recreates what it was like to be in the towers when the planes struck. It offered something solid and new, and it helped explain the frustrating mood of the rest of the novel. <br /><br />Could DeLillo have started with the crash? It might have explained the tone better in my mind, but he’d have been left without a clear ending. As it is, the attack does serve as a kind of climax.<br /><br />Even so, I realize that some of my distaste also came from being unwilling to let myself into the novel. Already, I was skeptical of the sensationalism of the event. 9/11 still reeks of press and politics, and making a film or writing a book about it seems like a path to an easy buck, even if it is Don DeLillo.<br /><br />As a treatment of 9/11, I appreciated Foer's <i>Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close</i> more. It gives us the distance from the attacks that time has allowed most readers. It takes us away from the immediacy of being in the towers and in the rubble, which most of us will never quite be able to relate to, and explores how that experience has become a part of our lives rather than the entirety of our lives.<br /><br /><i>Do I recommend it?</i> No. Maybe for someone who wants to know what it feels like to be lost.<br /><i>Would I teach it?</i> No.<br /><i>Lasting Impression:</i> Reading this is like walking through fog. There are shapes that look like people moving around you, but you never really know who they are.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17188318316528012202" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1246688954" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1246688954" />
    <a title="Lori " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26424937-lori"><img alt="Lori " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559993723p2/26424937.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1246688954?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lori " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lori " href="/user/show/26424937-lori">Lori </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416546061" href="/book/show/2439025.Falling_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26424937-lori?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1246688954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4825592251281506093">Never before have I returned days later to delete an entire review and rewrite it, even added a star, but that's the case with "The Falling Man." And it's because I'm still absorbing it and wrestling with it even though I finished days ago. I've never read DeLillo before and have had an unusual experience with this book in that I find the subtext more interesting than the text.<br/><br/>While reading I was questioning some of the decisions DeLillo made, especially one that I thought was a fail, the inclus</span>
  <span id="freeText4825592251281506093" style="display:none">Never before have I returned days later to delete an entire review and rewrite it, even added a star, but that's the case with "The Falling Man." And it's because I'm still absorbing it and wrestling with it even though I finished days ago. I've never read DeLillo before and have had an unusual experience with this book in that I find the subtext more interesting than the text.<br /><br />While reading I was questioning some of the decisions DeLillo made, especially one that I thought was a fail, the inclusion of Hammad, but days later I realize it was actually a perfect choice, forcing upon us that he is as human as anyone else. Main character Keith's poker game is a brilliant device to track Keith's life before and after he survives the towers. Some of the members of the weekly poker game don't survive, another is badly injured. Keith's new relationship with poker provides both an escape from reality and an honest forging of a new reality in whose confines he can feel safe again. <br /><br /><br />One could never "capture" 9/11 in a novel and if you're looking for that, there are documentaries and nonfiction books that come close. As a novel, "The Falling Man" is breathtaking, difficult, full of symbolism, a book to be haunted by. It's no easy read. I love that days after closing this book I'm still engaging with it and challenged by it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4825592251281506093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3097681993" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3097681993" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3097681993?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780330452236" href="/book/show/587922.Falling_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3097681993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer775240662166736436"><br/>Struggled to truly get into this, and had it not been for my strict rule of finishing a book once I've got pass the halfway point I would have likely abandoned it. Falling man will be the last 21st century DeLillo novel I will read, and it also made me realise that Cosmopolis wasn't so bad afterall.<br/>His 14th novel is an exploration of America's recent history, namely 9/11. DeLillo deploys a set of intersecting narratives which begins on September 11, 2001, just as the Twin Towers are falling.<br/>It s</span>
  <span id="freeText775240662166736436" style="display:none"><br />Struggled to truly get into this, and had it not been for my strict rule of finishing a book once I've got pass the halfway point I would have likely abandoned it. Falling man will be the last 21st century DeLillo novel I will read, and it also made me realise that Cosmopolis wasn't so bad afterall.<br />His 14th novel is an exploration of America's recent history, namely 9/11. DeLillo deploys a set of intersecting narratives which begins on September 11, 2001, just as the Twin Towers are falling.<br />It starts with New York City office worker Keith Neudecker who survives the attack, returning to his ex wife Lianne, and their young son Justin, instead of his own apartment, and in short, almost cryptic fragments that move around in time, we learn of the couple’s past difficulties and nominal reconciliation, in relation to Lianne’s troubled closeness to her elegant mother Nina, memories of her father, volunteer work with a Alzheimer’s patients’ support group, and the poker playing cronies with whom Keith has led a separate life with. DeLillo connects these and numerous other segments, including the figures of an Iraqi true believer preparing himself for martyrdom, a jaded European who predicts America’s impending downfall, and an eponymous performance artist whose daring suicidal plunges increasingly foreshadows and embodies the experience of free fall which the other characters are leaning towards. Falling Man is compassionately written, and constructed with a harrowing momentum that did occasionally keep my nerves on edge, but it's disconnected style and characters that really didn't do anything for me, left me feeling empty after closing pages. Falling Man for some could be seen as one of Delillo's better efforts in the last twenty years, but unfortunately for me it was just a chore to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="775240662166736436" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2501423714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2501423714" />
    <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen"><img alt="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560356189p2/47627265.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2501423714?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)" href="/user/show/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen">Kaylin (The Re-Read Queen)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=for-school">for-school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=finished-but-wish-i-hadn-t">finished-but-wish-i-hadn-t</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47627265-kaylin-the-re-read-queen?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2501423714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16021417491155665117"><b>1 Star</b> <br/><br/><i>Falling Man</i> is an epic failure largely because Don Delillo tries to tell a story that is <i>simply not his story to tell.</i> <br/><br/>Often lauded as the “first 9-11 novel” this story starts with our MC, Keith, literally on the streets of Manhattan after the second tower falls— already a risky and weighted choice for a narrator. <b>But then halfway through, Keith’s story is paralleled with Hammad’s, a man revealed to be one of the hijackers.</b> <br/><br/>And I <i>get</i> it okay. I <i>get</i> the whole parallel between Keith’s apath</span>
  <span id="freeText16021417491155665117" style="display:none"><b>1 Star</b> <br /><br /><i>Falling Man</i> is an epic failure largely because Don Delillo tries to tell a story that is <i>simply not his story to tell.</i> <br /><br />Often lauded as the “first 9-11 novel” this story starts with our MC, Keith, literally on the streets of Manhattan after the second tower falls— already a risky and weighted choice for a narrator. <b>But then halfway through, Keith’s story is paralleled with Hammad’s, a man revealed to be one of the hijackers.</b> <br /><br />And I <i>get</i> it okay. I <i>get</i> the whole parallel between Keith’s apathetic existence and loveless marriage and Hammad’s <i>intensely</i> driven and dedicated existence. I get what Delillo is trying to do, but it <b>falls dangerously flat and over-simplifies complicated people and a complicated tragedy. </b> None of the characters have very much substance, but portraying a terrorist as a singularly focused almost cartoon villain type felt especially lazy. <br /><br />Part of this oversimplification is the fault of the <b>needlessly vague post-modern style.</b> The whole avoiding-pronouns and descriptions has never been a tactic I’ve enjoyed. And the dialogue here was SO ridiculously on-the-nose and stilted. Example: <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>”You thought Keith would get you there.” <br />“What did I want?” <br />“To feel dangerously alive. This was a quality you associated with your father. But that wasn’t the case.”</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />Does any part of that feel like a conversation between a mother and daughter?? Particularly a conversation that just starts out of the blue about the daughters failing marriage?? <b>The narrative seems so focused on critiquing ‘hollow’ American family life, that the characters never feel like anything real— which makes the critique lose all credibility.</b><br /><br />I usually try to avoid speculating authorial intent (because let’s be real it’s pretty much irrelevant) but all of this feels <b>really rushed and bland.</b> And I can’t help but wonder if Delillo was in such a rush to get buzz for being the “first 9/11 novel” that no one stopped to consider if this was a story <i>worth</i> telling and if it did justice to the people involved. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16021417491155665117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2099437089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2099437089" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2099437089?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer6755690055185144789">The story of a dysfunctional family brought together by death and loss, <i>Falling Man</i> is emotional and gripping, and ultimately very inspirational.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150599279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150599279" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150599279?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780330452236" href="/book/show/587922.Falling_Man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=de-lillo-rama">de-lillo-rama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150599279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8911589570843806285"><b>CRITIQUE:</b><br/><br/><b>The Mystery Behind and Between Words</b><br/><br/>Up until <i>"Falling Man",</i> I was road-testing a theory that Delillo was fascinated by language, words and numbers, and the belief that behind and between words and numbers there is mystery and mysticism.<br/><br/>However, in this novel, he seems to have been more interested in what happens when we have no words to describe an event or an occurrence, when we are awestruck and literally speechless. <br/><br/><i>"Falling Man"</i> is one of DeLillo's two literary responses to 9/11, th</span>
  <span id="freeText8911589570843806285" style="display:none"><b>CRITIQUE:</b><br /><br /><b>The Mystery Behind and Between Words</b><br /><br />Up until <i>"Falling Man",</i> I was road-testing a theory that Delillo was fascinated by language, words and numbers, and the belief that behind and between words and numbers there is mystery and mysticism.<br /><br />However, in this novel, he seems to have been more interested in what happens when we have no words to describe an event or an occurrence, when we are awestruck and literally speechless. <br /><br /><i>"Falling Man"</i> is one of DeLillo's two literary responses to 9/11, the other being the Harper's essay, <a href="http://isu.indstate.edu/jakaitis/American%20Lit%20since%201945/Delillo%20-%20Reflections%20on%20Terror.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>"In the Ruins of the Future".</i>
</a><br /><br /><b>Before and After the Planes</b><br /><br />In the novel, we are witness to the accounts of two fictitious people who were in the Twin Towers and survived (Keith and Florence). There are other characters (including Keith's estranged wife, Lianne) who were not in either of the towers at the time of the plane crashes. <br /><br />The event marks a severance in time. For ever, time will either be before or after the (terrifying) day of the planes: <i>"That was before, this is after."</i><br /><br />For everybody who witnessed the event, it was dreadful, until people realised that the event wasn’t accidental, but a terrorist act. Initially, people couldn't believe that the act was real, they disbelieved it, but when the second crash occurred, they realised that it was actually an act of war against the U.S.A.<br /><br /><b>America's Irrelevance</b><br /><br />Martin, a progressive German art dealer (and the long-term lover of Lianne's mother), says:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>"We're all sick of America and Americans. The subject nauseates us...<br /><br />"For all the careless power of this country, let me say this, for all the danger it makes in the world, America is going to become irrelevant...<br /><br />"It is losing the centre. It becomes the centre of its own shit. This is the only centre it occupies."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><b>The Language of Religion</b><br /><br />Martin echoes the views of the Jihadists, perhaps because he might have been a Marxist radical in the 60's and 70's:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>"They speak of lost lands, failed states, foreign intervention, money, empire, oil, the narcissistic heart of the West...<br /><br />"They think the world is a disease. This world, this society, ours. A disease that's spreading...<br /><br />"They strike a blow to this country's dominance. They achieve this, to show how a great power can be vulnerable. A power that interferes, that occupies...<br /><br />"One side has the capital, the labour, the technology, the armies, the agencies, the cities, the laws, the police and the prisons. The other side has a few men willing to die...<br /><br />"Forget God. These are matters of history. This is politics and economics. All the things that shape lives, millions of people, dispossessed, their lives, their consciousness...<br /><br />"They use the language of religion, okay, but this is not what drives them...This is not an attack on one country, one or two cities. All of us, we are targets now."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><b>So Vast and Terrible, and Too Real</b><br /><br />In his essay, Delillo writes, <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>"The raw event was one thing, the coverage another. The event dominated the medium. It was bright and totalising, and some of us said it was unreal. When we say a thing is unreal, we mean it is too real, a phenomenon so unaccountable and yet so bound to the power of objective fact that we can't tilt it to the slant of our perceptions...<br /><br />"This was so vast and terrible that it was outside imagining even as it happened. We could not catch up to it. But it was real, punishingly so, an expression of structural limits and a void in one's soul..."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />We could not catch up with it quickly enough to formulate a suitable language to define, describe and discuss it.<br /><br /><b>Outrage at the Spectacle</b><br /><br />In the novel, a performance artist known as the <i>"Falling Man"</i> devises an act in which he dives head first from buildings and bridges (with an unseen safety harness). However, Americans aren't ready for him:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>"There were people shouting up at him, outraged at the spectacle, the puppetry of human desperation, a body's last fleet breath and what it held...The single falling figure that trails a collective dread...<br /><br />"He brought it back, of course, those stark moments in the burning towers when people fell or were forced to jump."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Not even membership of a crowd of spectators, the solidarity of a spellbound audience, could relieve the effects of the disturbance. As with the photo of the falling man, spectators don't want to imagine/learn/know what occurred the moment before the jump started or the moment after the jump came to an end, let alone what happened in between.<br /><br /><b>Intimacy after the Planes</b><br /><br />Ironically, despite the many relationship problems before the planes, afterwards we see greater or rejuvenated intimacy. The characters are beings who experienced an horrific event. Now they want to work out who they are and how they fit into the world and the people around them. <br /><br />This is almost DeLillo's attempt to map out the philosophical and metaphysical territory of being and event. Somehow, it manages to be resistant to language. Words are still not enough. Images, art and film play an increased role and function. DeLillo's characters (and, by extension, we readers) must find new ways of looking and seeing. <br /><br />Keith finds comfort in still life painting and in <i>"things he could not name."</i> Lianne realises <i>"there was something in the brushstrokes that held a mystery she could not name."</i> Either way, they still encounter and confront a mystery beyond language.<br /><br /><br /><b>VERSE AND WORSE:</b><br /><br /><br /><b>Women Running in the Morning Street<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />I like the streets.<br />This time of morning<br />There's something<br />About the city,<br />Down by the river<br />Streets nearly empty,<br />Cars blasting by<br />On the Drive.<br /><br /><br /><b>Simple as That<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />Go away,<br />Come back.<br />Simple as that.<br /><br /><br /><b>Trampled Under Foot</b><br /><br />Women without high heels<br />Holding up their skirts,<br />Trip in the crowd, trampled<br />Unless they get out first, <br />Water falling, water flowing<br />The pipes have all been burst,<br />A worker with a crowbar<br />The bent end held out first,<br />The equities trader pauses,<br />One of the ones who flirts,<br />The smoke turns the rest of us<br />Into such nervous introverts,<br />Men make improvised masks<br />Out of their torn off shirts,<br />Bottles of water pass up the line<br />To quench everybody's thirst.<br /><br /><br /><b>Seconds to Come<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />You are wishing<br />For death<br />And now <br />It is here<br />In the seconds<br />To come.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Final Jump of David Janiak,<br />Performance Artist and Dangling Man</b><br /><br />Structured<br />Improvisation.<br />Detailed and <br />Looming.<br />Headlong<br />Free fall.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Only Light<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />The only light<br />Was vestigial now,<br />The light of <br />What comes after,<br />Carried in the residue<br />Of smashed matter,<br />In the ash ruins of what<br />Was various and human,<br />Hovering in<br />The air above.<br /><br /><br /><b>But God<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />Did God<br />Do this<br />Or not?<br /><br /><br /><b>Rebut God<br />[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br /><br />God is<br />The voice<br />That says,<br />"I am<br />Not here."<br /><br /><br /><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><b>Led Zeppelin - "Trampled Under Foot"</b><br (Live)</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HGFITl5mFvs">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HGFIT...</a><br /><br /><b>Led Zeppelin - "Brandy &amp; Coke (Trampled Under Foot - Initial Rough Mix)"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=P9uTmnQn_4c">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=P9uTm...</a><br /><br /><b>Sister Sledge - "We Are Family"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://youtu.be/uyGY2NfYpeE">https://youtu.be/uyGY2NfYpeE</a><br /><br /><b>“A Quiet Photograph”: The Story Of “Falling Man”</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://allthatsinteresting.com/the-falling-man">https://allthatsinteresting.com/the-f...</a><br /><br /><i>This article contains a link to a video in which TIME discusses ‘The Falling Man’ with <div id="review_80724660" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80724660" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80724660?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2010 edition)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-non-core">1001-non-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80724660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9892497606306413181">You pick up a book. You read the blurbs. Those in front, at the back and perhaps those in the first few pages. Then you form an impression. Maybe this book is good. Maybe this is about this and about that. Then you pay for the book and start reading at home.<br/><br/>We all know about The Falling Man. On September 11, 2001, a man was photograph falling, or some people say flying, from the north tower. He appeared to have, in his last instants of life, embraced his fate. He departed from this earth like an</span>
  <span id="freeText9892497606306413181" style="display:none">You pick up a book. You read the blurbs. Those in front, at the back and perhaps those in the first few pages. Then you form an impression. Maybe this book is good. Maybe this is about this and about that. Then you pay for the book and start reading at home.<br /><br />We all know about The Falling Man. On September 11, 2001, a man was photograph falling, or some people say flying, from the north tower. He appeared to have, in his last instants of life, embraced his fate. He departed from this earth like an downward arrow. Five years after the attack, he was identified as <b>Jonathan Briley</b>, a a 43-year-old employee of the <i>Windows on the World</i> restaurant. <br /><br />This 2007 novel, <i>Falling Man</i> by the master storyteller <b>Don DeLillo</b>, is <u>not</u> about him.<br /><br />That may disappoint some people. I, too, was disappointed. I was already on the page 30 of this 246-page book waiting for that character to show up. The main male protagonist, <b>Keith</b> who works in the poker house in one of the towers, is seen in the opening scene walking - bleeding and scared and confused - aimlessly around the North Tower before it collapsed. So, where is the falling man? There is <b>a</b> falling man in the story but he was used by Mr. DeLillo as a symbol and not an active character. That falling man works in a circus or doing bungee jumps along the streets of New York.<br /><br />I also thought that Mr. DeLillo would dwell on another dysfunctional American family: Keith, Lianne and Justin as described in the blurbs. That was another disappointment. This is no <i>Home</i> by Marilynne Robinson, nor <i>Cost</i> by Roxana Robinson, nor any other Robinsons, not even funny like the movie <i>Meet the Robinsons</i>. And that is where the magic of DeLillo's writing is anchored upon: being unpredictable. He brings you to a place you are not expecting to go or to accept a reasoning that you are not expecting or not ready to believe. He seems to know what you are thinking as a reader and he hates to be double-guessed.<br /><br />As usual, writing is flawless. The characters, at times, felt like caricatures but I guess that it is the intention. Mr. DeLillo does not what the readers to focus on the family members. The story is about somethings bigger and looming above all of us: global terrorism, racial and religious beliefs and even the purpose of one's life. <br /><br />I learned something new though gruesome: <b>organic shrapnels</b> - human flesh that got driven into the skin of the survivor; it only shows up months later after the bombing.<br /><br />HEAVEN FORBID THAT YOU DIE WITHOUT READING THIS.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9892497606306413181" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3369762" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3369762" />
    <a title="Bart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/210421-bart"><img alt="Bart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547689631p2/210421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3369762?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bart" href="/user/show/210421-bart">Bart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers who find value in postmodern writing</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3369762" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1576137954787135396">Being clever, that's how DeLillo does it.<br/><br/><i>Falling Man</i>, a sparse work that is better than <i>The Body Artist</i> and much much better than <i>Cosmopolis</i>, does about as much as it can hope to do. Don DeLillo's powers simply aren't up to the task of making a new statement about a national tragedy like 9/11. He is an assembler of words and sentences and paragraphs and - at times - chapters, but he is not a thinker. What, then, has made him considered such an important voice in American letters?<br/><br/>Being clever, th</span>
  <span id="freeText1576137954787135396" style="display:none">Being clever, that's how DeLillo does it.<br /><br /><i>Falling Man</i>, a sparse work that is better than <i>The Body Artist</i> and much much better than <i>Cosmopolis</i>, does about as much as it can hope to do. Don DeLillo's powers simply aren't up to the task of making a new statement about a national tragedy like 9/11. He is an assembler of words and sentences and paragraphs and - at times - chapters, but he is not a thinker. What, then, has made him considered such an important voice in American letters?<br /><br />Being clever, that's how DeLillo does it.<br /><br /><i>Americana</i> and <i>White Noise</i>, much more than the oddly over-praised <i>Libra</i> and <i>Underworld</i>, are when DeLillo is at his very best. When he is able to write interesting sentences about unimportant things, when he is able to lend unserious topics the full focus of his rich prose, in other words, DeLillo is doing what he was called to do.<br /><br />But when he tries to use character-less personages to show us how important 9/11 was to New York's literary set, frankly, he's way beyond his talent's reach. Fundamentally, DeLillo is unable to lend gravity to a grave happening because such gravity would require characters that, in some way, resemble human beings.<br /><br />DeLillo doesn't seem to know any persons in real life, and so he assembles collections of phrases and limbs and quirky qualities - like a character who speaks only in monosyllabic words - and then subjects them to detailed scenery. DeLillo's characters belong to science fiction more than literary fiction - and in this way DeLillo seems to have become a lightweight Thomas Pynchon (which is fine; Pynchon can't be taken in more than small doses).<br /><br />Being clever, that's how DeLillo does it.<br /><br />In the last 10 pages of this 246-page book, there's a sentence that goes like this:<br /><br /><i>She was arguing with herself but it wasn't argument, just the noise the brain makes.</i><br /><br />This sentence more than any other may act as metaphor for DeLillo's recent works. Standing alone in its own paragraph, this sentence seems to say something important. But once a reader stops and looks at it from 360 degrees (what any artist should want), the reader realizes there's nothing there at all.<br /><br />Better put, <i>DeLillo is telling us a story but it isn't a story, just the noise a writer makes.</i><br /><br />When this unseriousness is married to DeLillo's usual tricks of repeating one clever phrase in stand-alone paragraphs throughout a chapter, what is left is a meaningless work by an artist whose fundamental lack of gravity has finally outrun his ample talent.<br /><br />Being annoying, that's how DeLillo did it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1576137954787135396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3263470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3263470" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/204626-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1184866379p2/204626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3263470?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/204626-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3263470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4594937281776543242">The thing with DeLillo is the what. The conversations. The sentence fragments. The writing style.<br/><br/>Of any list of candidates to write about the horrors of 9/11, DeLillo must have shown up. <i>Underworld</i> of course has the famous photo of the towers by Andre Kertesz. (<i>Falling Man</i> has another photo on its cover by Katie Day Weisberger. It is taken from the sky, where one sees a cyclopean vista of clouds but for the two towers peeking out, dwarfed. It's as breathtaking and emotive as the first, but with </span>
  <span id="freeText4594937281776543242" style="display:none">The thing with DeLillo is the what. The conversations. The sentence fragments. The writing style.<br /><br />Of any list of candidates to write about the horrors of 9/11, DeLillo must have shown up. <i>Underworld</i> of course has the famous photo of the towers by Andre Kertesz. (<i>Falling Man</i> has another photo on its cover by Katie Day Weisberger. It is taken from the sky, where one sees a cyclopean vista of clouds but for the two towers peeking out, dwarfed. It's as breathtaking and emotive as the first, but with our renewed perspective in the post-9/11 world.) His novel <i>The Names</i> focuses on a blind search for terrorists, or a group of politically motivated abductors in <i>Mao II</i>. Likewise, <i>Libra</i> places the reader into Lee Harvey Oswald's mindset. Adding in his New York experiences places DeLillo into the cast of authors who come to this subject matter with some creative authority.<br /><br /><i>Falling Man</i> seems at first to follow some regular DeLillo fare: a couple (Lianne and Keith) who had gone through a separation, and the clipped visuals of freeze-frame action. But as the novel bears down on the reader, they show greater depth and deeper pain. It's not about the day after. It follows them for what feels like the rest of their lives with 9/11 wrapped around their torsos. It captures the chronic trauma of it, and it leaves behind an emotional rift for an engaged reader. <br /><br />We've all got jobs that we'd have to get back to--even after a tragic event--pacing around carpeted cubicles or sitting behind desks and carrels. For me, through the din I could hear the rattling in his characters' souls. DeLillo doesn't break his own conventions or introduce brand-new style switches in the literature, but he'll jerk your stride and remind you of what some of us must live with.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4594937281776543242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It's not about initial trauma, but rather the subsequent unsettlement, an accounting of disheveled people living disheveled lives. <br/><br/>I'm unsure how to rate this book. On one hand, the author authentically conveys the shock and psychological disassociation that accompanies catastrophe. But on the other hand, it sometimes feels contrived, something that borders on exploitation. Is DeLillo opportunistic? I don't think so, at</span>
  <span id="freeText8540124034088864942" style="display:none">Aftermath. DeLillo's Falling Man isn't about 9/11, it's about the aftermath. It's not about initial trauma, but rather the subsequent unsettlement, an accounting of disheveled people living disheveled lives. <br /><br />I'm unsure how to rate this book. On one hand, the author authentically conveys the shock and psychological disassociation that accompanies catastrophe. But on the other hand, it sometimes feels contrived, something that borders on exploitation. Is DeLillo opportunistic? <div id="review_1032686025" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1032686025" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6041973-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598551141p2/6041973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1032686025?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/6041973-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6041973-josh?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1032686025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17359015116015900880"><b>”These are the days after.  Everything is now measured by after.”</b><br/><br/>As I write this, on the 13th anniversary of 9/11 -- one of the most tragic and revered days in American History -- I reflect back on Delillo’s “Falling Man” and what I got from this fictional text based on a time and place that is still fresh in many minds and hearts as if it happened, in a figurative sense, yesterday.<br/><br/>Outside of the death of loved ones and grief-stricken friends and family, “Falling Man” tells of a time after the t</span>
  <span id="freeText17359015116015900880" style="display:none"><b>”These are the days after.  Everything is now measured by after.”</b><br /><br />As I write this, on the 13th anniversary of 9/11 -- one of the most tragic and revered days in American History -- I reflect back on Delillo’s “Falling Man” and what I got from this fictional text based on a time and place that is still fresh in many minds and hearts as if it happened, in a figurative sense, yesterday.<br /><br />Outside of the death of loved ones and grief-stricken friends and family, “Falling Man” tells of a time after the tragedy.  How a family that was once broken becomes closer; an anonymous briefcase given back to its owner and a healing ensues between two people; the question of ‘why?’ and the reminiscence of a time prior to the incident; the symbolism of the street artist ‘The Falling Man’ disparate from who was labeled ‘Falling Man’; the point of view from both sides, enemy vs. foe.<br /><br />To be honest and literal, all of these thoughts, while being engrained in our minds at the time, slowly recede, grains of sand by hourglass, from our memory as we age and get older, looking at death in the mirror as it closely arrives to its destination, like Pearl Harbor is no longer on many minds until the anniversary.  <br /><br />Delillo’s story fails, at times, to share the emotional side of these people, but brings forth the emotion in the read itself.  This is my fifth book in two years by Delillo and it might be my last for awhile.  I’m sure there’s been better stories written about this topic, yet I can only recount what I’ve read from this one.<br /><br />Giving it a 3, but would give it a 2.5 if possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17359015116015900880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_105717736" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105717736" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105717736?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105717736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7922995745829170972">This novel has a great backdrop: the 9-11 and how it affected the lives of several interesting characters (even two of the 9-11 terrorists are here). But this was overwhelmed by a lot of tricks which didn't work (for me):<br/><br/>a. sparse dialogues, words and phrases used instead of complete sentences. Instead, for example, of one character (Keith) telling his estrange wife (Lianne) that she did something stupid quarreling with a neighbor with the latter's vicious dog beside her, he would just utter: "T</span>
  <span id="freeText7922995745829170972" style="display:none">This novel has a great backdrop: the 9-11 and how it affected the lives of several interesting characters (even two of the 9-11 terrorists are here). But this was overwhelmed by a lot of tricks which didn't work (for me):<br /><br />a. sparse dialogues, words and phrases used instead of complete sentences. Instead, for example, of one character (Keith) telling his estrange wife (Lianne) that she did something stupid quarreling with a neighbor with the latter's vicious dog beside her, he would just utter: "That dog" (no ellipsis even, just a final period: "That dog.", making it sound as if robots, not humans, were conversing);<br />b. vague philosophical musings that could either be, take a guess, profound or nonsensical ("God would consume her. God would de-create her."); and<br />c. sudden change in time and characters so that the reader seems to be constantly being flogged while trying to read. The author has no respect for the reader. With an abrupt change of character in the narration, for example, he could have very well lessened the reader's difficulty by naming the character suddenly in the scene. Instead, however, he uses mere PRONOUNS so the reader has to take the extra effort figuring out first who this "he" or "she" now is.<br /><br />Some novels would have stories so vivid, and characters so alive, that it's as if you are experiencing everything yourself (e.g., "Dangling Man" by Saul Bellow). This one by Don DeLillo, in contrast, is just like watching an old, poorly-directed film, in a black-and-white TV with a grainy screen and a very bad audio.<br /><br />What a torture!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7922995745829170972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2682803571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2682803571" />
    <a title="Lia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44005555-lia"><img alt="Lia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578446760p2/44005555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2682803571?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lia" href="/user/show/44005555-lia">Lia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2682803571&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2682803571" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5465784293546322533">I started out hating this book; but this is turning out to be extremely thought-provoking. I hope to keep updating my notes on this as I process, and maybe revisit some of Delillo’s more political works (or maybe other new ones.)<br/><br/>This book starts out torturously slow, and confusing. You don’t know who is related to whom, or what is happening. You’re vaguely aware of this pervasive fog of confusion immediately after the 9/11 attack. Delillo jumps from character to character without any transition </span>
  <span id="freeText5465784293546322533" style="display:none">I started out hating this book; but this is turning out to be extremely thought-provoking. I hope to keep updating my notes on this as I process, and maybe revisit some of Delillo’s more political works (or maybe other new ones.)<br /><br />This book starts out torturously slow, and confusing. You don’t know who is related to whom, or what is happening. You’re vaguely aware of this pervasive fog of confusion immediately after the 9/11 attack. Delillo jumps from character to character without any transition or contextualizing. To make it worse, you don’t get any distinctive linguistic tics that help you tell the interior monologues of Uncle Charles from Dedalus. They all more or less sound the same.<br /><br />The confusing snail pace gradually accelerates to a very tense “ending,” <b>which is actually the beginning.</b> This is the kind of books that demand to be reread... like Finnegans Wake. (I’m not kidding, Delillo is a Joyce fan, and the association is pretty weak, but I thought I saw some textual allusions to FinWake as well.) <br /><br />After the 9/11 attack, a dazed cast gropingly finds ways to deal with the wound as though suspend in a fog, slowly, very slowly falling, declining, acquiesced. They’re entrapped in a cycle of ... well, not exactly terror, just constant helpless decline. Individuals submit to old age, to religion, to infidelity, to gambling, to Alzheimer’s, mostly without a fight, or they fight those who jolt them out of their mindless existence, those whom are insensitive enough to remind them of their trauma, to reopen wounds. The things they encounter, their thoughts, their coping are so disjoint, so dislocated, so insular, I wanted to abandon the book.<br /><br />But time is not linear in this book, the infuriatingly slow descent accelerates into the big bang that started it all — at the very end, we get a glimpse of the terrorist’s existential thoughts as he crashes into the Tower; which quickly cuts into Keith and others who got jolted out of their average everydayness, and struggle to survive the inferno, or else witness their mates brutally killed amidst the confusion. The terrorist welcomes his death, he thinks it brings him closer to eternity, to God. This is the end for him. For the rest of the cast, this is simply the beginning of their aimless, confused, slow decline. <br /><br />The plot is simple enough; but I think what this book asks us to consider is what arts and language <i>can</i> do in face of hypercapitalism, inequality, deep resentments, insular thinking, radicalism, ennui, aging, mental decline, meaninglessness. If poetry is barbaric after Auschwitz, then what is a novelist to do after 9/11? Can the event be represented with language or photographs or performance arts or music or paintings <i>at all</i>? [In case if you’re wondering, I don’t have an answer. I think Delillo is trying to say something about that, but I need time to figure out what.]<br /><br />Lianne’s father committed suicide to escape Alzheimer’s; Lianne runs a writing workshop for a group of patients coping with Alzheimer’s. Lianne is blunt about it — there’s no therapeutic value, it saves no one, they will continue to decline, this will not stave off the disease.<br /><br />Through Lianne’s project, Delillo seems to be signaling his own awareness of the limitation of arts and narratives. <br /><br />Lianne also assaults a neighbor who plays some kind of foreign music that could be Greek or Middle Eastern; the neighbor explains the music represents peace to her; but Leanne accuses her of being insensitive to traumatized people after the event. She calls her music “noise” and she’s provoked. I initially read that as representing xenophobia, and maybe it does, but as I read on, it seems to signal a pervasive walling-off response, people want to be left alone and allowed to drift along unseeing, unhearing. They don’t want still life paintings that remind them of the Twin Towers; they don’t want musics that sound vaguely middle eastern, they don’t want performance artists or photographs that represent the man who jumped. There’s a consensus of indecency and indictment against artistic response to process, to cope, to generate dialogue. <br /><br />Lianne ultimately turns to the Church (but not the faith):<br /><br /><blockquote>“She thought that the hovering possible presence of God was the thing that created loneliness and doubt in the soul and she also thought that God was the thing, the entity existing outside space and time that resolved this doubt in the tonal power of a word, a voice.<br />God is the voice that says, “I am not here.”<br />She was arguing with herself but it wasn’t argument, <b>just the noise the brain makes.</b>”</blockquote> <br /><br />There’s irony in the fact that she dismissed her neighbor’s (perhaps religious/ devotional) music as noise, years later, she gradually accepts the decline of her own life, her own aging, her own ennui, and she turns to a tone, a word, a voice — which she calls a noise — but that’s what she turns to. She wants safety, she can’t have safety, and this is all she can get.<br /><br />What’s Delillo’s verdict? Can words give us anything after such a “fall”? Or are they merely ... White Noise?</span>
  <a data-text-id="5465784293546322533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_6829568" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6829568" />
    <a title="tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/230113-tim"><img alt="tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458012692p2/230113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6829568?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tim" href="/user/show/230113-tim">tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6829568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17000429631542109169">In some ways, DeLillo seemed the perfect candidate to write a novel about 9/11. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11762.White_Noise' title='White Noise by Don DeLillo' rel='nofollow noopener'>White Noise</a> there was the idea of terrorists flying a hijacked passenger jet into the White House. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11761.Underworld' title='Underworld by Don DeLillo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Underworld</a>, the construction of the twin towers lumed large in the background for a good part of the book. The cover photo itself focuses on the twin towers rising into clouds (smoke?), with a bird (a plane?) flying close by. It may be a stretch to read a connection with 9/11 into Underworld's cover (not to mentio</span>
  <span id="freeText17000429631542109169" style="display:none">In some ways, DeLillo seemed the perfect candidate to write a novel about 9/11. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11762.White_Noise" title="White Noise by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">White Noise</a> there was the idea of terrorists flying a hijacked passenger jet into the White House. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11761.Underworld" title="Underworld by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Underworld</a>, the construction of the twin towers lumed large in the background for a good part of the book. The cover photo itself focuses on the twin towers rising into clouds (smoke?), with a bird (a plane?) flying close by. It may be a stretch to read a connection with 9/11 into Underworld's cover (not to mention it has nothing to do with DeLillo's writing), but it was hard not to imagine these connections while in the middle of reading Underworld when 9/11/01 happened. Moreover, many aspects of DeLillo's writing deal with major world-changing events such as 9/11. How drastically the world changed after JFK's assassination is a topic that threads through several of his books (most magnificently in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400.Libra__Contemporary_American_Fiction_" title="Libra (Contemporary American Fiction) by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Libra</a>). <br /><br />And so maybe not unsurprisingly, immediately following 9/11 DeLillo was accosted by people who thought, for some reason, he would have answers to why 9/11 happened. He responded by writing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28703.Cosmopolis" title="Cosmopolis by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Cosmopolis</a>, a book which passed over the topic of 9/11 altogether and instead tells the story of a billionare stock market genius engaged in the intentional sabotage of his personal assets as well as those of his clients--all of which occurs over the course of one day in the year 2000 (how quickly everything can change in a day). It is a story of our own undoing from the inside, as opposed to our undoing by outsiders (bringing into question our role as the unprovoked victims of foreign terrorism). <br /><br />Letting a few more years pass, DeLillo finally took on what many thought he eventually would, despite high expectations difficult for anyone to live up to.<br /><br />To be fair, I didn't want to give in to these expectations. I wanted and tried to read Falling Man because it is DeLillo, not because it's a novel about 9/11. Yet after reading Falling Man, I felt something missing in the fragments of the super-charged dialog, vivid settings, and empty characters tossing about in my head--because like the sooty, scattered tons of paper from the fallen World Trade Center, DeLillo's fictional fragments were nearly devoid of emotion. This may very well have been DeLillo's intention, however, that the reader walk away just as vacant and lost as the shells of his characters living on after 9/11. <br /><br />For me, the incredible dialog DeLillo often creates as well as the way he writes about art and history are reasons enough to read any of his books. Yet these reasons will not be enough for everyone. Ultimately, if you feel compelled to read Falling Man--whether because it is a book by DeLillo, or simply because it is about 9/11--you should. Although, I will recommend that it not be the only book you read written by DeLillo. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17000429631542109169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34356518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13929643440922737545">Although I understood that the writing style was fractured to reflect the fractured lives of the characters, I found the style annoying and frustrating. Though the topic was interesting, the author would switch from character to character and it was hard to figure out what was going on. In the beginning I would keep going back and looking for clues in the text so I could figure out which character's story I was on, but it became so annoying that I gave up and just would read, not always knowing </span>
  <span id="freeText13929643440922737545" style="display:none">Although I understood that the writing style was fractured to reflect the fractured lives of the characters, I found the style annoying and frustrating. Though the topic was interesting, the author would switch from character to character and it was hard to figure out what was going on. In the beginning I would keep going back and looking for clues in the text so I could figure out which character's story I was on, but it became so annoying that I gave up and just would read, not always knowing which character we were on. I got that it was probably a reflection of the mindset after 9/11, that it all blended together and felt empty, but I just felt that this could have been communicated in a better fashion for the reader. The story is depressing and fractured. An interesting topic and manner of handling it, but just not to my taste. I think if the writing had been clearer or more engaging for the reader, I might have loved this book. Others might really enjoy it, particularly the more intellectual types - the english lit majors. (Yes, Katy Lain, I am talking to you)</span>
